Abstract
Surveying And Mapping, LLC (SAM) conducted a marine archaeological survey and diver probing of 12
anomalies for the proposed North Jetty Sand Search Project located on the north side of the Galveston
Jetties in Galveston County, Texas. The project is located in Texas state waters and spans portions of
State Mineral Lease Tracts 196, 197, 198, 201 and 202 just outside of the entrance to Galveston Bay. The
area of potential effect (APE) measures approximately 3,200 x 10,300 feet and encompasses 554 acres.
Water depths range from 9 to 20 feet below Mean Lower Low Water. Dredging will be limited to within
14 feet of the existing seafloor grade. Suitable sediment from this area will be used for habitat
restoration projects on Bolivar Peninsula. This project is funded under The Coastal Impact Assistance
Program by Galveston County with additional contributions from funds administered by the Texas
General Land Office through the Coastal Erosion Planning and Response Act. This archaeological
assessment was completed under a subcontract with HDR Engineering, Inc. (HDR) of Corpus Christi,
Texas. SAMâs field investigations included a marine geophysical survey, performed under Texas
Antiquities Permit 7560, between February 17 and 27, 2016. An archaeological assessment was
conducted of all data acquired from the survey. The purpose of SAMâs survey was to locate potential
archaeological sites so they can be avoided by dredging. A desktop review of the cultural background
determined that 12 archaeological investigations have been reported and 5 archaeological sites have
been confirmed within 3 miles of the APE including 41GV127, 41GV143, 41GV144, 41GV146 and the
Steamship City of Waco. One magnetometer survey, reported by Arnold (1987) overlapped the APE;
however, no wrecks were confirmed in the area. At least 142 shipwrecks have been reported from
historic and/or cartographic sources to potentially lie within 3 miles, including as many as 18 within the
APE. SAM initially recommended avoidance of 26 geophysical targets, all of which have associated
magnetic anomalies, including 7 wrecks confirmed by sonar imagery, 6 magnetic anomalies associated
with ambiguous sonar contacts, and 13 magnetic anomalies without sonar contacts. Twelve unidentified
anomalies were probed by divers from July 21-26, 2016. Nine anomalies are recommended for dredging
clearance based on probing results, including one where partial remains of a shrimp trawler were
discovered. Buried wooden wrecks were discovered at 3 other anomalies. Seventeen targets, including
10 confirmed wrecks, remain potentially eligible for the State Antiquities Landmark or for the National
Register of Historic Places. All 17 targets (Mag 1/Sonar 1, Mag 2/Sonar 2, Mag 3, Mag 4, Mag 5/Sonar
15, Mag 7, Mag 8/Sonar 3, Mag 9/Sonar 4, Mag 10, Mag 11, Mag 13, Mag 15/Sonar 6, Mag 16/Sonar 7,
Mag 17/Sonar 8, Mag 20, Mag 23/Sonar 9 and Mag 24) are recommended for avoidance by bottomdisturbing activities associated with this project. This study was completed in compliance with Section
106 of the National Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470) and the Antiquities Code
of Texas (Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191). The minimum reporting and survey
requirements for marine archaeological studies conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are
mandated by The Texas Administrative Code, Title 13, Part 2, Chapters 26 and 28, respectively.

vi | P a g e

I.

Introduction

Surveying and Mapping, LLC (SAM) conducted a marine geophysical survey and archaeological
assessment of the proposed North Jetty Sand Search Project (Figure 1). This project is funded under The
Coastal Impact Assistance Program by Galveston County with additional contributions from funds
administered by the Texas General Land Office (GLO) through the Coastal Erosion Planning and
Response Act. SAMâs field investigations consisted of a marine geophysical survey, performed under a
subcontract with HDR Engineering, Inc. (HDR) of Corpus Christi, Texas. The area of potential effect (APE)
encompasses 554 acres on the north side of the Galveston Jetties just outside of the entrance to
Galveston Bay. The APE is located in Texas state waters and spans portions of State Mineral Lease Tracts
196, 197, 198, 201 and 202. Water depths range from 9 to 20 feet (ft) below Mean Lower Low Water
(MLLW). Suitable sediment from this area will be used for habitat restoration projects on Bolivar
Peninsula. Cultural resources investigations were required by the Texas Historical Commission (THC)
because dredging activities might affect historic cultural resources resting on or embedded in the
seafloor. Dredging will be limited to within 14 ft of the existing seafloor grade.
Geophysical survey was completed between February 17 and 27, 2016. Probing of selected targets was
conducted by SAM and T&T Subsea, LLC between July 21 and 26, 2016. The purpose of this study was
to assess the archaeological potential of the APE, so sensitive sites can be avoided by bottom
disturbance activities related to dredging. An archaeological assessment was conducted of all data
acquired from the survey, including areas slightly beyond the APE. No artifacts were collected during this
survey.
Archaeological sites, should they be present, might be eligible for nomination to the National Register of
Historic Places (NRHP) or as State Antiquities Landmarks. Archaeological sites in this context typically
would be shipwrecks or abandoned watercraft. The potential for shipwrecks in a given area is expected
to correlate with the amount of ship traffic utilizing the area. The APE was heavily navigated by
watercraft prior to construction of the northern jetty from 1893 to 1898. Prior to jetty construction, the
APE straddled the seaward margin of the Galveston Outer Bar, which was a particularly hazardous area
for ships entering Galveston Bay. Following completion of the northern jetty, the APE and surrounding
areas are believed to have been used for abandonment of decommissioned vessels, including perhaps
aged remnants of the merchant sailing fleet.
A desktop review of the cultural background determined that 12 archaeological investigations have
been reported and 5 archaeological sites have been confirmed within 3 miles of the APE including
41GV127, 41GV143, 41GV144, 41GV146 and the Steamship City of Waco. One magnetometer survey,
reported by Arnold (1987) overlapped the APE; however, no wrecks were confirmed in the area. At least
142 shipwrecks have been reported from historic and/or cartographic sources to potentially lie within 3
miles, including as many as 18 within the APE.
Analysis of the survey data revealed 26 magnetic anomalies consistent with those produced by complex
anomaly sources such as shipwrecks. Seven of those anomalies are confirmed as wrecks by sonar
1|Page

imagery, while another 6 anomalies are associated with ambiguous sonar contacts that might be
associated with buried wreckage. Thirteen magnetic anomalies were selected as potential shipwrecks
that lack sonar contacts. Twelve unidentified anomalies were probed by divers from July 21-26, 2016.
Nine anomalies are recommended for dredging clearance based on probing results, including one where
partial remains of a shrimp trawler were discovered. Buried wooden wrecks were discovered at 3 other
anomalies. Seventeen targets, including 10 confirmed wrecks, remain potentially eligible for the State
Antiquities Landmark or for the NRHP. SAM recommends avoidance of the 17 remaining targets by
bottom-disturbing activities associated with this project, including Mag 1/Sonar 1, Mag 2/Sonar 2, Mag
3, Mag 4, Mag 5/Sonar 15, Mag 7, Mag 8/Sonar 3, Mag 9/Sonar 4, Mag 10, Mag 11, Mag 13, Mag
15/Sonar 6, Mag 16/Sonar 7, Mag 17/Sonar 8, Mag 20, Mag 23/Sonar 9 and Mag 24.
The results reported in this document will support Galveston Countyâs application for a United States
Army Corps of Engineers (USACE) Permit, requesting permission to dredge sediment from the APE for
various future habitat restoration projects on Bolivar Peninsula. This study was completed in compliance
with Section 106 of the National Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470), which
requires that federally funded or permitted projects give due consideration to cultural resources, and
with the Antiquities Code of Texas (Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191), which provides
for the protection of cultural resources on state lands. The APE is publicly owned and administered by
the Texas GLO; therefore, a Texas Antiquities Permit was required prior to beginning fieldwork. Work
was completed under Texas Antiquities Permit 7560. The minimum reporting and survey requirements
for marine archaeological studies conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The
Texas Administrative Code, Title 13, Part 2, Chapters 26 and 28, respectively.

2|Page

Figure 1: Project Location and Proposed Transects

This report includes maps showing magnetic contours, side-scan sonar imagery and probing results. The
report is organized into five sections that provide the necessary context for interpreting the results of
the survey. Section II summarizes the physical environment of the APE based on a combination of
published literature and data collected by this survey. Section III describes the archaeological
background of the area within a 3-mile radius of the APE, including relevant prehistoric and maritime
history of the area, previous archaeological investigations nearby, and the potential for occurrence and
preservation of prehistoric and historic sites in the APE. Section IV describes the survey design and the
methods used for acquisition, processing and analysis of geophysical and probing data. Section V
presents an archaeological assessment of the geophysical survey and probing investigations. Finally, a
list of all bibliographic references cited in the text is provided as Section VI. Appendix A (not for public
disclosure) contains maps of the survey results. Appendix B (not for public disclosure) contains side-scan
sonar target descriptions and locations. Appendix C (not for public disclosure) contains a table of
magnetic anomaly and wreck locations recommended for avoidance due to their potential historic
nature. Appendix D contains a copy of Texas Antiquities Permit 7560 and the THC concurrence letter (in
the final draft only).

3|Page

II.

Physical Environment

The APE is located in the Gulf of Mexico 0.9 to 2.8 miles offshore. The near seafloor geology of this area
was influenced by sea level changes occurring during and since the Late Wisconsin Glaciation. At that
time significant amounts of water were locked up in continental glaciers. Sea level has risen more than
300 ft since the last glacial low stand, about 20,000 to 22,000 years ago. Many sea level curves have
been published showing minor variations in the timing and degree of change (e.g. Figure 2).
Nevertheless there is broad consensus in the magnitude and approximate timing of this event. The
former Pleistocene/Holocene landscape, now submerged on the continental shelf, is of particular
interest to archaeologists, because the timing of its most recent exposure overlaps the period of human
habitation in North America; however, the Pleistocene-aged surface and ancestral Trinity River bay-head
deltaic deposits are located below the depth of proposed dredging for this project (Wallace et al. 2010).
During the historic period, the APE was situated at the seaward margin of the Galveston Bay Entrance
Outer Bar. This is a natural shoal formed from sediments deposited by the ebb tide from Galveston Bay
and reworked by waves from offshore. Water depth in the modern APE ranges from 9 to 20 ft MLLW.
This is not substantially different than depths during the late 19th century when the jetties were
constructed; suggesting that much of the sediment in the APE might be a remnant of the Galveston
Outer Bar as it existed at the beginning of the 20th century. Once the north jetty was completed in 1898,
the APE was blocked from deposition of sediments transported by the Galveston Bay ebb tide and, at
the same time, was protected from erosion by the prevailing southeasterly winds.

4|Page

Figure 2: Holocene Sea Level Curves by Various Authors
Reproduced from Weise, et al. (1980, Figure 16)

III.

Archaeological Background

Prehistory
Evidence from many archaeological sites in North and South America suggest that the New World was
colonized by humans as early as 15,000 Before Present (BP), predating the Clovis Culture by at least
2,000 years. The Paleo-Indian Period, sometimes referred to as the Big Game Hunting Tradition,
continued until about 10,000 BP. Waters and Stafford (2014) suggest that the earliest projectile points
diagnostic of the Paleo-Indian Period, the Clovis complex of fluted-blade technology, likely evolved in
the New World long after migrants had already spread throughout both North and South America. The
idea of a pre-Clovis migration from central Asia, possibly as early as 16,000 BP, is supported by genetic
evidence and correlates with an apparent decline in megafauna populations beginning shortly after that
time.
Stright (1990) summarized inundated sites discovered along the Gulf of Mexico coast at depths up to 59
ft below Mean Sea Level. For example, artifacts from all Paleo-Indian traditions with the exception of
Folsom have been found along a 22-mile stretch of McFaddin Beach near Sabine Pass, Texas (Long 1977;
cited in Stright 1990). The collection includes 14 Clovis Points. Faunal material recovered there includes
5|Page

a wide variety of Pleistocene species, such as mammoth, mastodon, saber-toothed cat, bear, giant
armadillo, bison, tapir and horse. An elephant tusk from the site yielded a radiocarbon date of 11,100
+/- 750 BP. The former Pleistocene land surface at McFaddin Beach, known regionally as the Beaumont
Clay Formation, is about 5 ft below sea level.
Many known inundated sites have been discovered as a result of dredging. For example, human remains
and artifacts were recovered from the Texas City Channel in Galveston Bay near the ancestral Trinity
River Valley (Aten and Good 1985). The site contained 4,000 bone specimens from Pleistocene species,
such as horse and tapir, including 42 bones that appeared to have been modified, and a variety lithics
interpreted as stone tools.
Few attempts have been made to actively seek intact buried site deposits on the continental shelf.
Nevertheless, one such effort by Coastal Environments, Inc. (1986) located two possible prehistoric shell
midden sites in vibracore samples collected near Sabine Pass at depths of 54 and 59 ft below Mean Sea
Level. Pollen analyses demonstrated that both deposits had formed under subaerial conditions;
however, the core sample sizes were too small to allow a definitive determination of cultural origin for
the shell deposits.

Potential for Submerged Prehistoric Sites
There is little doubt that humans once lived along Gulf of Mexico coastlines long-since submerged by
rising seas. Many such sites are presumed to have been destroyed by wave energy during the process of
inundation. The search for intact sites has focused on landforms that both are recognizable on acoustic
sub-bottom profiles and are known to have attracted prehistoric populations. The most likely locations
for such sites to have been preserved are along streams where burial in alluvial deposits might have
afforded protection from wave energy. As river valleys flooded to become estuaries, deltaic sediments
may have accumulated on top of already sealed deposits, providing further protection by the time those
sites were exposed to the open Gulf of Mexico. Remnants of flooded and buried stream channels that
were above sea level during the period of human habitation in North America are considered the most
probable locations for prehistoric sites to occur on the submerged continental shelf. The presence of
fresh surface water and the ecological diversity of an ancestral delta would have been attractive to
prehistoric human populations while affording the potential for protection of sites from the destructive
force of sea level rise; however, the APE overlies a deeply incised portion of the ancient Trinity River
valley, thus there is no potential for intact prehistoric cultural deposits in the APE.

Maritime History
European ships first entered the Gulf of Mexico in 1508, sixteen years after Columbusâ first voyage,
when Sebastian de Ocampo sailed around the island of Cuba (Weddle 1985:21). In 1519, the same year
that Hernando Cortez arrived in Vera Cruz on his way to conquering the Aztec empire, another Spaniard,
Alonso Alvarez de Pineda, became the first to explore the Gulf Coast of Texas. Pineda led an expedition,
under orders from the governor of Jamaica, to explore and map lands bordering upon the Gulf of
Mexico in hopes of discovering a passage to India.

6|Page

Pinedaâs voyage eliminated any idea of a natural route to Asia through the Gulf of Mexico but also
produced detailed knowledge of wind and current patterns that would help determine the best sailing
route between Vera Cruz and Havana (Chipman 1992:24-26). The Spanish silver fleet built upon this
knowledge to conduct steady trade between Vera Cruz and Europe via Havana from the mid-16th century
through 1790. Wind and currents generally dictated one of two routes from Vera Cruz to Havana, either
crossing the central Gulf of Mexico or following a northern route closer to the coast (Lugo-Fernandez et al.
2007). The northern route would have occasionally brought Spanish flotillas near the APE during storms, as
witnessed by the wrecks of silver fleet ships near Port Mansfield, Texas and on the Louisiana Coast.
Cabeza de Vaca was the first European credited with landing on the Texas Coast. De Vaca was one of only
four survivors to reach Mexico City from an ill-fated expedition led by PÃ¡nfilo de NarvÃ¡ez in 1527. Cabeza
de Vaca and about 80 other Spaniards from the expedition landed on or near Galveston Island on rafts.
Those men who managed to survive the first winter were enslaved by Native American tribes including the
Karankawa and Coahuiltecan. Cabeza de Vaca and three other survivors escaped and wandered from tribe
to tribe through Texas, northern Mexico, and down the Pacific Coast of Mexico, eventually reaching
Mexico City after about 8 years. Cabeza de Vaca published a narrative of his experiences among the native
peoples of the new world in 1542 after returning to Spain (e.g., Cabeza de Vaca 2013).
The Spanish crown did little to further its interests along the Texas Coast until the French began to
explore the area. RenÃ© Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle arrived in Matagorda Bay in 1685 with three
ships and 300 colonists. La Salle had intended to settle on the Mississippi River; however, he misjudged
the riverâs position and arrived instead on what is now the Texas Coast. There he attempted to establish
a permanent colony, which he christened Fort St. Louis. Unfortunately one of their three ships,
LâAimable, was lost on arrival while trying to enter the bay through Pass Cavallo. Soon thereafter, a
group of colonists returned to France in one of their two remaining ships. La Salle then led a series of
overland expeditions in an attempt to locate the Mississippi River but was murdered by disgruntled
members of his crew. During their absence from Fort St. Louis the only remaining ship, La Belle, drifted
aground and was lost inside Matagorda Bay. Cut off from Europe, most of the remaining inhabitants of
Fort St. Louis, already greatly diminished in numbers, eventually perished (Weddle 1991).
La Salleâs expedition was unsuccessful; however, the mere presence of the French invigorated Spanish
interest in the region. Several expeditions were mounted to search for the French settlement, which was
eventually discovered, abandoned, by Alonso de LeÃ³n in 1689. Subsequent expeditions investigated the
feasibility of a Spanish settlement, which was finally accomplished in 1721 when La BahÃ­a del EspÃ­ritu
Santo was established at the site of Fort St. Louis (Weddle 1991), although it was moved four years later
to a location on the Guadalupe River.
Jean Baptiste BÃ©nard de La Harpe is credited with producing the earliest detailed map of Galveston Bay
in 1721. La Harpeâs goal was to reestablish La Salleâs abandoned fort on Matagorda Bay, but unknown to
him at the time, he had arrived at the wrong bay. Sailing with La Harpe was Francois Simars de Bellisle.
Bellisle had been an officer on a French ship, MarÃ©chal dâEstrÃ©e that stranded near the entrance to
Galveston Bay in 1719 (Folmer 1940). Bellisle was the sole survivor of a small group of officers who
elected to stay ashore after their ship was freed. He was a slave to Native Americans for over a year
7|Page

during which time he wandered Coastal Texas from Galveston Bay to Fort Saint-Jean-Baptiste at
present-day Natchitoches, Louisiana (Folmer 1940).
Texas lacked permanent European settlements until 1746 (Zavaleta 1986:131). Concerned that French
colonists in Louisiana would move into the area, the King of Spain appointed Jose de Escandon in 1746 to
colonize Nuevo Santander (Pierce 1917:17). He successfully established several towns and missions along
the Rio Grande, although none of his settlements were located near the modern Texas coast. Galveston
Bay received its modern name in 1783 when JosÃ© de Evia surveyed the bay and christened it GÃ¡lvezton,
in honor of Louisiana governor Bernardo de GÃ¡lvez (Davis 2005:306).
Early settlement of Galveston was spurred by the conflict over land rights in Texas between the United
States, Spain and New Spain, which was fueled by the Louisiana Purchase in 1803. Galveston Island and
Point Bolivar became a base of operations for privateers, backed by French, Mexican and AngloAmerican interests seeking to overturn Spanish control of the region. Louis-Michel Aury established a
base on the island in 1816 amid the Mexican War for Independence. He organized the first government
of Galveston with backing from Mexican supporters in New Orleans, proclaimed the new town an
official port of the Mexican Republic, and began raiding Spanish shipping. Within a year Aury gathered
over 700 troops on the island and led an attack on Mexico.
Jean Lafitte took over Auryâs fort at Galveston during the latterâs time in Mexico. James Long declared a
Texas Republic in 1819 with Lafitte as governor of the island. Lafitte was forced from the island in 1821
by the United States government as a result of a stricter policy against piracy in the Gulf of Mexico
(Davis 2005:419â420). James Long established a rival fort on Point Bolivar in 1820, and rebuilt Lafitteâs
fort in Galveston after it was largely destroyed by Lafitte upon leaving.
In 1824 Stephen F. Austin requested that the newly independent Mexican government settle Galveston
in order to aid in the shipment of cotton to England. Austin had just founded the town of Matagorda at
the mouth of the Colorado River in 1822. The Congress of Mexico established a port of customs at
Galveston in 1825 (Cotham 1998:1; Francaviglia 1998:91, 95). Galveston was not incorporated as a city
until late in 1836 on the heels of Texas Independence from Mexico. By 1838 Galveston had grown to be
the largest source of cotton shipped to the East Coast and Europe. By the beginning of the Civil War
Galveston was the second largest city in Texas and by 1870 it was the largest (Cotham 1998:184).
The USACE began experimenting with various jetty materials in 1874, hoping that the channel would
deepen if the tidal flow could be forced through a narrower opening. Early attempts at jetty
construction met with failure until the current design was implemented and completed. The south jetty
was built from 1887-1893 and the north jetty from 1893-1898. The new jetties reportedly had the
desired effect deepening the outer bar to 25 ft (Alperin 1977:41â55, 100). Subsequent increases in
depth could only be maintained through periodic dredging beginning in 1922 (Ward 1993:24) and
continuing to the present.
The Galveston Hurricane of 1900 completely destroyed roughly a third of Galveston, severely damaged
the rest, and killed one sixth of the population. Galveston had been considered the fourth wealthiest
city in the country prior to the storm (Fay 1900). The City of Galveston never recovered to its full former
8|Page

prominence; however, Galveston Bay has continued to increase in shipping volume steadily throughout
the twentieth century. Most commercial shipping traffic has shifted to Houston and smaller amounts to
Texas City, but regardless of the point of origin, the volume of traffic through Bolivar Roads and past the
APE has continued to increase. Today Houston alone is ranked first among United States (U.S.) ports in
foreign tonnage.

Potential for Historic Shipwrecks
SAM researched potential shipwrecks in areas offshore of the entrance to Galveston Bay reported
within 3 miles of the APE (Table 1). Sources consulted for this research included the Texas
Archaeological Sites Atlas, maintained by the THC, the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration
(NOAA) Automated Wreck and Obstruction Information System (AWOIS) database, a shipwreck
database compiled by PBS&J, and historic maps from the Texas Historical Overlay (Foster et al. 2006).
The Sites Atlas contains shipwrecks reported from historic records. The AWOIS list focuses on
shipwrecks and other navigational hazards along the coastal U.S. The PBS&J database was assembled by
a group of archaeologists, including this author, and is based in part on information gathered from
charts, historical reports, THC files, and the AWOIS database. The PBS&J database focuses primarily on
well-documented commercial wrecks postdating 1850.
The THC Sites Atlas was searched out for a distance of 3 miles in each direction from the survey
boundary. It is important to note that the final deposition of most wrecks has not been ascertained.
Historical accounts of wreck site locations are often inexact and can contain errors based on the original
source. Precise locations of most wrecks are unknown. Shipwrecks for which no position estimate is
available, but which, based on their descriptions, might have been lost in the vicinity, are included in
Table 1.
Wrecks reported between or south of the jetties or specifically associated with geographic features
located outside of the project area, such as the South Jetty, Galveston Bay or the Gulf of Mexico, have
been excluded from Table 1 unless represented as nearby in THC records. Wrecks charted inside or on
the margin of the survey area by at least one source are indicated by use of an asterisk (*) in Table 1.
None of the ships reported in Table 1 have been located by archaeologists, rather these are potential
identifications that might be associated with wrecks discovered by this survey.
At least 142 shipwrecks have been reported within a 3-mile radius of the survey area by one or more of
the sources listed above. The positioning for most of these reports is generally accurate only at the scale
of large landmarks. For example, any wreck reported as occurring on the âGalveston Outer Barâ or
âGalveston Bay Entranceâ might be in the survey area. Of this number, as many as 18 wrecks (likely
including some duplicates) have been reported within or on the margins of the APE (indicated by an
asterisk [*] in Table 1).

Galveston Bar

Factors Affecting Vessel Loss
The main factors influencing the loss of vessels in the APE are the presence of the Galveston Outer Bar,
the concentration of vessel traffic through the bay entrance, and weather. Historic navigation through
this area may have begun as early as the 16th century. Stranding is the most common mechanism by
which ships are wrecked, accounting for about 64 percent of total losses reported by the U.S. Lifesaving
Service for the period 1876 through 1914 (Gearhart, et al. 1990: Volume IV, 59-61). Two thirds of
stranding events were caused by weather. Most stranding events occur along a shoreline; however,
stranding (or grounding) on sandbars at the mouths of harbors was also common. Stranding is the most
likely source of shipping losses, other than perhaps intentional abandonment, in the APE.
Foundering is another likely source of shipwrecks in the APE. The Annual List of Merchant Vessels of the
United States (United States Department of the Treasury 1906-1946) defines foundering as casualties
due to leaking or capsizing of vessels. Waves from offshore tend to be exaggerated in both height and
steepness over harbor bars causing them to break. This effect is compounded when the wave direction
is opposed by an outgoing tide. Vessels caught near a harbor bar during stormy or foggy conditons, or
even at night, could quickly find themselves in jeopardy. Foundering historically accounted for about 6
percent of total losses, almost half of which were caused by weather. Although foundering was
relatively uncommon, vessels that foundered were less likely to be saved than those experiencing any
other type of incident. Fifty-four percent of all vessels that foundered were totally lost (i.e., sunk). By
comparison, 46 percent of strandings, 24 percent of other causes, and only 5 percent of collisions
resulted in a total loss (Gearhart 1990: Volume IV, 59-61).
Prior to completion of the Galveston Jetties in 1898, the APE straddled the seaward margin of the outer
bar (Figures 3 and 4). This is the point at which inbound ships would first encounter water too shallow
for navigation if they strayed beyond the channel. The channel was not maintained by dredging prior to
1922, and it meandered significantly over time spans as short as a single season. Ship captains entering
Galveston Bay relied on the knowledge of local pilots to help them safely negotiate the entrance channel
and to avoid running aground on the bar. Based on the position of the bar, one would expect at least the
western third of the APE to be an area where ships were apt to run aground prior to completion of the
jetties. Even after the jetties were built (Figure 5), sailing ships lacking auxiliary power or the assistance
of a tug boat had an elevated risk of running aground. Once the jetties were completed, areas outside of
the jetties became isolated from normal navigation. As a result, the area north of the jetties may have
become a convenient location for abandonment of decommissioned vessels after 1898. In fact, this area
was designated as a âDumping Groundsâ on government navigation charts from 1962 (U.S. Department
of Commerce 1962) until 1977 (NOAA 1977). Regulations and permission for dumping in the area,
according to a note on each chart, were available from the office of the District Engineer at the USACE. It
is unknown by the author whether abandonment of vessels was allowed under USACE regulations at
that time.

Figure 3: APE in 1867 (USCGS 1867)

Figure 4: APE in 1877 (Howell 1877)

Figure 5: APE in 1899 (USCGS 1899)
Severe weather was, and remains, the most significant environmental factor contributing to shipwrecks,
accounting for 55 percent of total losses reported by the U.S. Lifesaving Service for the period 1876
through 1914. Prior to modern weather forecasting, mariners were hampered by relatively short
warnings of approaching storms. Hazardous weather conditions can occur along the central Texas Coast
throughout much of the year. Strong northern winds are generated through the area each winter by the
passage of 15 to 20 northers, or rapidly moving polar fronts, each generally lasting 24â36 hours. Such
storms may contain 50-mile-per-hour winds and generate intense wave activity and longshore currents
(McGowen 1976:19â23, 94). Hurricane season lasts from late June through October. Hurricanes have
winds in excess of 74 miles per hour and can have devastating effects on any ships caught in their paths.
Table 2 summarizes tropical weather events that have affected Galveston over the course of its history
(information, as summarized in Borgens et al. 2011, from Brownsville Herald 1987, Galveston Daily News
1886, Paine and Morton 1986, and Roth 2000; updated from Roth 2010).

Factors Affecting Vessel Preservation
Preservation of vessels wrecked within the APE would depend largely upon the composition of their hull
and the degree to which they become and remain buried. The lower portions of vessels wrecked in this
region can become buried soon after sinking due in part to scour and liquefaction of sediments during
storms. Metal vessels remain exposed much longer than wooden ones, although their lower hulls are
equally susceptible to burial.
Any wooden components that are not buried tend to disintegrate quickly. Wood-boring organisms
rapidly consume exposed wood. In shallow water, wood weakened by biological organisms and water
saturation is then disarticulated and laid flat or removed by hurricanes and fishing trawlers. In areas
such as this having copious quantities of weakly consolidated sediment, significant portions of a wooden
hull can become buried, protecting it from biological degradation. Burial produces an oxygen-deprived
environment, which promotes long-term preservation of wood.
Iron artifacts, on the other hand, corrode five times faster in seawater than when buried on land.
Calcium carbonate forms a concretion around iron artifacts that incorporates surrounding materials
such as rock and sand or even other artifacts. Oxidation can eventually leach out most or all of the iron,
leaving only a carbonate mold of the original artifact (Hamilton 1998). Nevertheless, iron and steel hulls
can survive exposure to seawater in various states of preservation for well over a century.
21 | P a g e

Previous Investigations
A number of marine archaeological surveys have been completed within 3 miles of the APE. SAM
reviewed the findings of previously published cultural resource studies having relevancy to the present
project. The western 3,200 ft of the APE were included within a much larger magnetometer survey
conducted by the Texas Antiquities Committee (now the THC) in 1980 (Arnold 1987). The purpose for
their survey was to examine areas having some of the highest potential for historic shipwrecks along the
Texas Coast. In the process, they hoped to locate candidate wrecks of historic significance that were
known from historic records to be present in the vicinity of the Galveston Bay Entrance. Four survey
areas were covered, which together encompassed almost 17 square miles. The magnetometer was
towed along parallel transects spaced 50 meters (m) (164 ft) apart. Their 1,800-acre Bolivar Peninsula
Survey Area detected 109 magnetic anomalies, including several in the present survey area. Exact
positions of anomalies from their survey are unavailable, so itâs not possible to precisely correlate those
results with anomaly locations from the present survey. No wrecks were confirmed within the North
Jetty APE, although one anomaly in the area was interpreted as a âpossible wreckâ based presumably on
its correlation with a wreck that appears at that location on modern NOAA charts. This is the only prior
archaeological survey to have included portions of the North Jetty APE.
A remote-sensing survey was conducted south of the Galveston Jetties by the National Underwater and
Marine Agency (NUMA) and the Texas Antiquities Committee from 1986-1988 (Oertling, et al. 1992).
The purpose of their survey was to search for the Texas Navy Warship, Invincible. The schooner
grounded at the entrance to Galveston Bay in 1837 and was a total loss. The shipâs location has yet to be
confirmed.
In 1991, Espey, Huston & Associates (EH&A) completed an archaeological remote-sensing survey along
the Houston Ship Channel (HSC) for the USACE, Galveston District (Hoyt 1992). That projectâs objectives
were to identify significant shipwrecks and other historical resources within Galveston Bay that might be
impacted by future USACE undertakings. The projectâs survey corridor measured over 40-miles long and
included a linear corridor from the inner end of Bolivar Roads to the Beltway 8 Bridge near Houston, as
well as a 3.6-mile section of the Galveston Ship Channel. That project resulted in a listing of over 3,400
magnetic anomalies and 570 sonar targets.
EH&A completed extensive archival research for the USACE in 1992 (Foster et al. 1993), as a supplement
to the project reported by Hoyt (1992). Foster et al. defined areas of potential archaeological and
historical sites within and adjacent to the HSC. As a result of that research, 103 previously recorded
archaeological, historical, and shipwreck sites and over 600 other potential historic sites were reported
within 1 mile of the HSC. EH&A compiled that information into a database containing over 500 possible
shipwreck sites and made recommendations regarding particular sites and portions of the study area
warranting further research.
EH&A conducted a sub-bottom profile survey over 10 geophysical anomalies along the Galveston Bay
Entrance Channel that had been previously recommended for archaeological dive investigations (Hoyt
1993). The sub-bottom data were also assessed regarding the potential for subaerial Pleistocene

22 | P a g e

landforms that might be conducive to the occurrence and preservation of prehistoric archaeological
sites.
In 1994, EH&A undertook a series of cultural resource investigations for the USACE to study potential
cultural resource localities along the HSC (Hoyt et al. 1998). That project included, in part, a close-order
magnetometer survey of selected, previously defined areas along the HSC between the Beltway 8 Bridge
and the outer end of the Galveston jetties. Results of those investigations revealed 242 historic site
localities along the HSC. All localities were identified by name, type, age, potential archaeological
integrity, potential NRHP eligibility, and research priority status. In addition, several hundred magnetic
anomalies were identified during the remote-sensing survey. Based on a correlation of anomalies with
suspected historic site localities, recommendations were made for field examination of potential sites
associated with the old Lynchburg town plat, the San Jacinto town plat, the industrial/commercial areas
on San Jacinto River (near Old River), the Bayport Channel area, Red Fish Bar, and the Galveston Jetties.
The Institute of Nautical Archaeology (INA) conducted dive investigations of the steamboat Denbigh, a
Civil War blockade runner, during 1997 and 1998 (Arnold et al. 1999). The site is located near the
Galveston North Jetty. Denbigh ran aground near the end of the war while trying to enter Galveston Bay.
INAâs project summarized research into the shipâs history and design, as well as details of what was
learned from remote sensing and from diving on exposed portions of the site. The ship has been
designated as Texas Archaeological Site 41GV143.
PBS&J conducted a remote-sensing survey of an area known as Big Reef near the Galveston South Jetty
in 2002 in order to assess the potential for historic shipwrecks. The area was proposed as a source of
sand for beach nourishment. Three magnetic anomalies were investigated and cleared from further
concern (Jones et al. 2003). Portions of a disarticulated wooden shipâs keelson were discovered washed
onto the shore. This timber was not associated with other remains or anomalies and was considered to
have washed into the area from elsewhere.
PBS&J conducted a marine archaeological assessment in 2004 for the Texas Department of
Transportation (TxDOT) at the proposed expansion of the Bolivar Ferry Terminal (Gearhart et al. 2006).
Magnetic anomalies were probed from a surface boat, with assistance from Coastal Environments, Inc.,
to determine whether they were associated with buried wrecks. PBS&J archaeologists, with assistance
from TxDOT divers, investigated one anomaly that was too deep to effectively probe from the surface.
No significant cultural sites were discovered.
PBS&J conducted a marine archaeological assessment in 2007 for TxDOTâs proposed Galveston-Bolivar
Causeway (Borgens et al. 2007). Their geophysical survey encompassed 2,747 acres including a large
contiguous area between Bolivar Peninsula and Pelican Island and a smaller area west of Pelican Island.
They recommended 47 magnetic anomalies for archaeological avoidance. One of these is believed
associated with a submerged Civil War fortification. The others are potential shipwrecks.
PBS&J conducted marine archaeological assessments of three proposed sand borrow areas for the Texas
GLO (Borgens et al. 2011). The 518-acre South Jetty Sand Source was surveyed in 2008. This area is
located in a similar setting, with respect to the Galveston Outer Bar, as the North Jetty APE. Two other
23 | P a g e

areas, Anchorage Areas B and C totaling 527 acres, were surveyed in 2009 and 2010 near the inshore
end of the Galveston Jetties. Fourteen magnetic anomalies were recommended for archaeological
avoidance at the South Jetty Sand Source. An additional 5 anomalies were recommended in the
anchorage areas. Four anomalies were later probed in the South Jetty area resulting in confirmation of
two wrecks and clearance of two locations from further concern.
A marine archaeological assessment of 67 acres was performed in 2012 for a proposed TxDOT expansion
of a dredge placement area to be used for material from the Bolivar Ferry (Miller et al. 2012). The survey
was conducted by AmaTerra Environmental, Inc. and Southeastern Archaeological Research, Inc. No
potential historic sites were detected by their survey, although one significant anomaly recommended
by Borgens et al. (2007) was confirmed nearby within the original placement area footprint.
One National Register District, Fort Travis Seashore Park, exists within 3 miles of the APE. Fort Travis was
designated as a State Antiquities Landmark in 1984 and was listed on the NRHP in 2005. The fort was
built and added to between 1898 and 1943 and defended the entrance to Galveston Bay during both
world wars. Gun emplacements at the fort included batteries Thomas Davis and Rudolph Ernst (Alperin
1977: 208). During World War II, German prisoners of war were housed at the fort (Darst 2010). The site
was sold to private interests in 1949 and was purchased by Galveston County for a public park in 1973.
Four shipwrecks have been recorded as archaeological sites within 3 miles of the APE. These include
41GV127, 41GV143, 41GV144 and 41GV146. A fifth historic wreck, the Steamship City of Waco, has
been located in the area but has not been assigned an official site number on the THC Sites Atlas. Site
41GV127 was first reported in 1991 as part of NUMAâs search for the Texas Navy ship Invincible
(Oertling, et al. 1992). The site is a late-19th-century ship constructed of wood and covered below the
waterline in Muntz metal sheathing. Site 41GV143 is the Civil War blockade runner Denbigh mentioned
above (Arnold et al. 1999). Site 41GV144 is the wreck of an early-20th-century ferro-concrete tanker.
The site was reported in 1998 by INA as partially exposed on the north jetty (Arnold 1999). Site 41GV146
is the wreck of a 20th-century concrete ship discovered by INA in 1998 near the beach on Bolivar
Peninsula.
The Steamship City of Waco was first relocated by a NOAA multi-beam sonar survey in 2001. NOAA
provided details to the USACE who miss-identified the wreck as the dredge Galveston based on its size
and limited information (email correspondence from Steve Hoyt to Robert Gearhart, June 6, 2003). In
2003, this author surveyed over the wreck using side-scan sonar and magnetometer. It was immediately
determined that the vesselâs location corresponded to a wreck buoy on late-19th-century charts (e.g.,
USCGS 1899), thus likely was not associated with the dredge Galveston, which was lost more recently.
Subsequent correspondence with Steve Hoyt at the THC suggested the City of Waco as a likely
candidate, which matched the dimensions observed on sonar records. The City of Waco was a steamship
that burned and sank on November 9, 1875, killing all 53 persons on board, while anchored beyond the
outer bar waiting for weather to improve, so it could safely enter the bay. Additional survey data
collected on City of Waco in 2012 through a partnership with NOAA under THCâs annual permit 2035
demonstrated that the site was more exposed than when first discovered (Borgens 2013).

24 | P a g e

IV.

Research Design

Survey Methods
SAMâs survey methodology followed the requirements stipulated by the THC. The survey was conducted
from a 25-foot hydrographic survey vessel. Positioning was provided by a Hemisphere VS131 Differential
Global Positioning System (GPS). Hypack software was used for navigation and for logging of GPS-sensor
offset positions, as well as magnetometer and bathymetry data. Horizontal positions were based on the
Texas State Plane Coordinate System (North American Datum 1983 [NAD83], South Central Zone, U.S.
ft). Primary transects were surveyed parallel to one another and spaced an average of 20 m (66 ft) apart.
Bathymetry data were collected using an Odom CVM recording fathometer (200 kiloHertz). The CVM
was calibrated using an Odom Digibar Pro sound velocity meter. Bathymetry is intended only as a
planning tool in the event of future archaeological activities and is a requirement of the Texas
Antiquities Permit. Bathymetry has not been tidally corrected or illustrated in this report.
Magnetic data were acquired using a Geometrics 882 magnetometer equipped with a depth sensor. The
magnetometer sensor was towed on the sea surface 72 ft aft of the survey boat and within 20 ft of the
seafloor. Magnetometer data illustrated in Appendix A has been thinned to a 1-second interval between
data points.
An Edgetech 4125 side-scan sonar (600 kiloHertz) was towed from the survey vesselâs starboard side.
GPS positions were integrated into sonar imagery using Edgetechâs Discover Sonar Acquisition Software,
which was also used for recording the acoustic data. The sonar was set to image the seafloor for a
distance of 50 m (164 ft) to either side of the survey path. A mosaic image of the sonar data (Section V
and Appendix A) was created using Chesapeake SonarWiz software. The data were analyzed to locate
and map objects projecting above the seafloor.
The magnetometer was the primary instrument relied upon for locating and recognizing potential
shipwreck sites. Side-scan sonar imagery provided verification of magnetic anomaly sources exposed
above the mud line. Shipwrecks are visible on the side-scan sonar record if exposed and are located
solely based on magnetometer readings if buried. Vessels predating World War II tend to be constructed
of wood thus generally are not preserved above the seafloor. Nevertheless buried portions of such sites
can retain a high level of artifact preservation and historic integrity. No artifacts were collected during
this survey.
A Microsoft EXCEL spreadsheet was used to process the magnetometer data prior to contouring. The
spreadsheet uses an algorithm to remove diurnal fluctuations. The difference between anomalous
values and the local background field results in a dataset where abnormally high and low magnetic
values (anomalies) are centered around zero (the ambient level). This process removes low frequency
trends from the dataset, such as those caused by diurnal variation, geologic gradients, or gradual
changes in water depth while leaving high-frequency variations (anomalies) intact. Diurnal processing
allows visual representation of the data that reflects the polarity of magnetic anomalies.

25 | P a g e

A contour map of the diurnally-corrected magnetometer data was prepared using Bentleyâs GeopakÂ®
digital terrain modeling software at a 5-nanoTesla (nT) contour interval (Appendix A). Magnetometer
data illustrated in Appendix A and text figures below has been thinned to a 1-second interval between
data points. Magnetic amplitudes between +5 nT and ï­5 nT are considered insignificant for purposes of
marine archaeological interpretations. The 0-nT contour level is omitted from the contour maps to
prevent a cluttered appearance. Positive amplitude in all magnetic contour illustrations is indicated by
red contours. Negative amplitude is indicated by blue contours.

Interpretation of Magnetometer Data
The probable sources for most magnetic anomalies are relatively small items of modern ferrous debris.
Such anomalies tend to be more concentrated in proximity to modern navigation channels and in other
areas of heavy use. Ferromagnetic debris is common in such settings due to the frequency of
commercial and recreational boat traffic. Concentrations of magnetic anomalies also tend to occur in
the vicinity of industrial developments, petroleum wells, and pipelines.
The method used here to interpret unidentified magnetic anomalies is based upon a comparison of
verified shipwreck and debris anomalies as detailed in Gearhart (2011) and summarized below. The
author has assembled, over the past several years, a diverse collection of magnetic anomalies recorded
over 29 verified shipwrecks and many debris sources in an effort to characterize differences between
these two categories of magnetic sources. Shipwrecks included in this dataset encompass a wide variety
of hull compositions (wood, iron, steel, and concrete), propulsion systems (sail, steam-driven
paddlewheels and propellers, oil and diesel screws, and towed or pushed barges), ages (mid-16th to the
mid-20th century), and depositional environments (harbor entrances, surf zones, beaches, marsh, oyster
reefs, open bay, and the Gulf of Mexico). These vessels also wrecked in various ways including by
stranding, foundering (sinking), boiler explosions, and burning. Some were even partially demolished
and/or salvaged after wrecking. Based on Gearhartâs (2011) sample, it is possible to characterize
shipwreck and debris anomalies in an empirical fashion.
Complexity
Many archaeologists have attempted to characterize the differences between shipwreck anomalies and
those associated with ferromagnetic debris. Shipwreck anomalies frequently have been described as
appearing âmulticomponentâ or âcomplexâ, emphasizing the fact that shipwreck anomalies, in certain
contexts, may exhibit multiple dipoles in addition to having unpaired monopolar amplitudes. Garrison et
al. (1989: II, 223) summarized the more common methods used at the time for prioritizing anomalies.
Historic shipwreck anomalies were said to share the following characteristics: multiple amplitude peaks
of differing magnitudes spread over an area greater than 10,000 square meters (2.5 acres), gentle
gradients, and a linear association with anomalies on adjacent transects. Debris anomalies were
characterized as typically having single amplitude peaks covering an area of less than 10,000 square
meters, steep gradients, and no alignment of anomalies on adjacent lines.
Horizontal Dimensions
The horizontal size of an anomaly is referred to by many archaeologists as âdurationâ in reference to
how many seconds a magnetometer registers anomalous readings as it passes overhead in the water
26 | P a g e

column. Duration obviously depends upon the velocity of the survey boat; therefore, this author instead
refers to duration as anomaly width exceeding the +/ï­ 5-nT amplitude level. Duration is often used by
archaeologists as a tool for discriminating potentially significant anomalies from those more likely to be
caused by debris. For example, Linden and Pearson (2014) would consider an anomaly significant if it
has amplitude of at least 50 nT and a duration equivalent to 65 ft or more.
The widths of historic wood-hulled shipwrecks and debris anomalies overlap significantly, thus width (or
duration) is not a very useful tool for discriminating between the two categories of anomaly. Most
debris anomalies tend to be no wider than anomalies observed over wood-hulled sailing ships or woodhulled steamboats; however, within the category of shipwreck anomalies, there is a 15-fold difference in
width between the smallest wood-hulled sailing vessel and the largest steel tanker. In other words,
when it comes to width alone, anomalies of small wooden ships, which tend to be the most historic,
look more like debris anomalies and bear less resemblance to larger, especially ferrous, shipwrecks.
The most difficult shipwrecks to detect are the small, wooden, generally historic sites, precisely because
their anomalies are in the size range of many debris anomalies. The smallest verified shipwreck anomaly
known to this author (Figure 6) was recorded over the wreck of an early 19th-century sailing vessel, Site
41CL92. The site was shown by divers to measure at least 23 x 52 ft (7 x 15.9 m) and is characterized by
a large collection of concreted artifacts, iron bar stock, and pig iron ballast (Borgens 2004).
Distinguishing the anomalies of small shipwrecks from debris anomalies in the same size range is
complicated and cannot be done on the basis of size alone. Garrison et al. (1989: II, 223) characterized
shipwreck anomalies as typically being larger than 10,000 square meters; however, if that were the only
criterion used for selecting significant anomalies, all of the wood-hulled sailing ships and all but one of
the wood-hulled steamboat anomalies known to this author would be excluded. For example, Site
41CL92, believed to be either the 67-ft New Orleans schooner Hannah Elizabeth that sank in 1835 or the
Mexican naval sloop General Bustamante that sank in 1830 (Borgens 2004), covers an area of only 1,580
square meters (0.4 acres) out to the 5-nT contour.
Amplitude
Amplitude is even less useful as a tool for differentiating shipwrecks from debris due to the substantial
overlap between shipwreck and debris anomaly amplitudes. The peak-to-peak amplitude of shipwreck
anomalies from Gearhart (2011) averages 270 nT for wood-hulled sailing vessels (n=6); 5,020 nT for
wood-hulled machine-powered vessels (n=7); and 10,386 nT for iron- and steel-hulled vessels (n=12).
Debris anomalies can have amplitude virtually anywhere within the range of shipwreck anomalies.
Anomaly amplitude correlates poorly with vessel size because amplitude depends greatly upon the
distance between the magnetometer and the magnetic source. Anomalies with large amplitude can
result from a wooden shipwreck containing a relatively small iron mass when measured at close range.
Orientation
Despite the diversity of shipwrecks, their anomalies consistently have one characteristic in common with
one another. They are aligned closely with earthâs magnetic field (e.g., Figure 6). In mid-latitudes of the
northern hemisphere, where all of the wrecks discussed by Gearhart (2011) occur, the primary negative
27 | P a g e

pole of each shipwreck anomaly is located to the north of its positive pole. By contrast, the alignments
of debris anomalies are not limited to one particular orientation.
A simple debris source produces an anomaly dominated by its permanent magnetic field, thus the
anomaly alignment associated with a solitary item of debris is determined by the orientation of the
object on the seabed, and the polar axis of debris anomalies can be oriented along any point of the
compass. On the other hand, shipwrecks are complex anomaly sources comprised of many
ferromagnetic objects. The randomly-oriented permanent magnetic field of each ferromagnetic
component in a shipwreck tends to be cancelled by the overlapping fields of other nearby sources. The
weaker, earth-induced field of each source is reinforced by the induced fields of all other nearby
components, since all of the induced fields are oriented parallel to earthâs local field lines. As a result,
shipwrecks and other complex sources have anomalies closely aligned to the direction of magnetic
north.

Figure 6: Example of a Verified Shipwreck Anomaly, Site 41CL92
The characteristic of anomaly orientation is used to differentiate magnetic anomalies caused by most
ferrous debris from anomalies caused by shipwrecks. Consideration of magnetic anomaly orientation
has potential to eliminate up to about 80 percent of debris anomalies from further archaeological
28 | P a g e

concern. By chance, about 20 percent of simple debris sources have anomaly orientations overlapping
those of complex sources, including shipwrecks. At present there is no consistent method for eliminating
that 20 percent of debris from consideration as a potential buried shipwreck.
Many anomalies, however, can be eliminated from consideration as potential shipwrecks simply by
demonstrating that their orientations differ substantially from the direction of magnetic north. The only
cases in which anomalies of simple debris sources should align with earthâs magnetic field is when, by
chance, their magnetic moments, as determined by their orientation on the seafloor, closely align with
magnetic north. The opposite case, a wood-hulled shipwreck having a magnetic anomaly not aligned
with magnetic north, is virtually impossible, as it would require a large percentage of a wreckâs many
ferromagnetic components, by chance, to have the same magnetic moment.
Interpretation of magnetic anomalies, in practice, was done by comparing the magnetometer data,
contoured at a 5-nT interval, to the anomaly of the small wood-hulled historic shipwreck, 41CL92, shown
in Figure 6. The verified 41CL92 shipwreck anomaly was contoured using the same parameters as the
survey data from this project and was properly oriented and scaled to match the data to which it was
compared. Any anomalies lacking a polar orientation similar to 41CL92, given a sufficient data density,
were excluded from further consideration. Anomalies remaining as shipwreck candidates were then
researched with the Railroad Commission of Texas to determine which ones could be attributed to
abandoned petroleum wells, which also happen to closely resemble shipwreck anomalies.

Probing Unidentified Anomalies
Antiquities Permit 7560 was amended to include probing investigations in consultation with the State
Marine Archaeologist (email exchange between the author and Amy Borgens on May 2, 2016). Twelve
magnetic anomalies were investigated further to determine whether any are associated with buried
shipwrecks. This task was accomplished by divers probing a grid pattern across the central portion of
selected anomalies. Commercial divers from T&T Subsea, LLC were hired to perform the probing under
the full-time, direct supervision of the Principal Investigator. Diving was conducted from the MV Petite
(Figure 7), a 59 x 79-foot liftboat. The liftboat was positioned by the Principal Investigator using a
Hemisphere, dual-GPS heading system and Hypack navigation software. A combination of navigation
guidance at the helm and directions provided to the captain by the Principal Investigator ensured that
the bow of the liftboat was centered on each probing grid at a sufficient distance to avoid affecting
anomaly sources when jacking the dive vessel out of the water.
Probing was accomplished using a combination of hydraulic and solid steel probes. Hydraulic probing
used an 18-foot iron pipe connected to a large water pump. The proposed minimal horizontal limits of
probing at each anomaly were determined prior to diving based on analysis of magnetic data. The
probing grid was modified as warranted by circumstances. Sample points selected for probing typically
were spaced 10 feet apart on a square grid. Sediments were probed to a minimum depth of 14 feet
below the seafloor at each of 12 anomalies to verify the presence/absence of buried shipwrecks. In
practice, deep probes having negative results penetrated to a depth of 16 feet (up to the handle of the
probe).

29 | P a g e

The central point of each search grid was located by a diver navigating directly forward of the bow for a
measured distance (using a knotted line tied to a clump weight deployed below the bow). The search
grid center was marked by a steel T-probe. The locations of other grid points were determined from the
center point by a combination of compass navigation and pacing pre-determined distances along
measured arcs using a knotted circle-search line for guidance. (Note: Several attempts were made to
map probes using GPS on a buoy towed by the divers; however, multi-path issues, caused by the
imposing height of the nearby liftboat, rendered the GPS buoy unreliable.)
Probes penetrated the seafloor to a depth of 16 feet below the natural seabed unless a wreck or other
anomaly source was encountered. Once a suspected wreck was discovered, the pre-set probing pattern
was abandoned and efforts focused instead on delineating the dimensions and depth of burial of the
wreckage. Probes on and around a confirmed anomaly source penetrated the sediment far enough to
establish the top and edges of the source. Additional probes to a depth of 14 ft or greater were placed
at the discretion of the Principal Investigator.

Figure 7: Liftboat MV Petite

30 | P a g e

V.

Results

Twenty six geophysical targets were initially recommended for avoidance, based on the survey results,
due to their potential association with historic shipwrecks. Seven of those targets were confirmed as
wrecks or abandoned vessels by sonar imagery, while another 6 targets were associated with ambiguous
sonar contacts that might indicate buried wreckage. Thirteen magnetic anomalies that lack sonar
contacts also were selected as potential shipwrecks. At the request of the project sponsor, 12 magnetic
anomalies were investigated by probing in hopes that avoidance restrictions could be minimized,
thereby increasing the area of sediment available for environmental restoration projects. As a result of
diver probing, 9 of the original 26 geophysical targets, including the partial remains of a shrimp trawler,
have been recommended for cultural resource clearance and 3 additional wrecks were discovered.
Findings of the geophysical survey and diver probing are detailed below.

Side-Scan Sonar
A total of 21 unique targets were observed in the sonar data (Table 3). Side-scan sonar data is illustrated
as a mosaic in Figure 8 and in Appendix A (not for public disclosure). Most of those targets were imaged
multiple times. Appendix B (not for public disclosure) contains a complete catalog of sonar target
images, including all 143 unique views observed. Selected views of sonar targets are illustrated below
adjacent their corresponding magnetic anomalies. Dimensions and interpretations of sonar targets are
provided in Table 3.
A total of 7 wrecks are visible on sonar imagery (Sonar Targets 1, 2, 4, 6, 7, 8 and 9). Five sonar targets
appear to be rock piles (Sonar 16, 17, 18, 19 and 20; Appendix B). Rock was also observed adjacent a
sunken barge (Sonar 9). All of the targets containing rock are relatively close to the north jetty,
suggesting that they might be associated with the jettyâs construction. One target (Sonar 10) resembled
a pipe-frame mast commonly used on shrimp trawlers to support outriggers and handle nets. Probing
later proved this identity. Four sonar targets (indicated by * in Table 3) were investigated by probing in
July 2016, and are now recommended for cultural resource clearance.

Magnetometer
Magnetometer data is illustrated as contours in Appendix A. Additional data were gathered over
selected anomalies along transects spaced 10 m (33 ft) apart in order to better evaluate their historic
potential. Information regarding petroleum infrastructure was obtained from the Railroad Commission
of Texasâ Public GIS Viewer. No wells or pipelines are reported in the APE, and no magnetic anomalies
were observed that would suggest the presence of petroleum infrastructure in the area.
Twenty six magnetic anomalies were initially interpreted as potential historic shipwrecks based on their
resemblance to verified wreck anomalies (e.g., Figure 6), as described in Section IV. Seven of those
anomalies were confirmed as wrecks based on sonar data. The other 19 anomalies were interpreted as
potential wrecks based primarily on their magnetic signatures. The possible association of those 19
anomalies with historic shipwrecks could not be ruled out based on the geophysical data at hand. In the
absence of further information, their sources were considered to potentially meet criteria for inclusion
on the NRHP. Probing was recommended to the project sponsor as a means of possibly eliminating
archaeological avoidance restrictions associated with some of those anomalies. Twelve magnetic
31 | P a g e

anomalies (indicated by * in Table 4), including 4 associated with sonar targets, were investigated by
probing in July 2016. Buried wrecks were discovered at 3 of those anomalies, while 9 others, including
partial remains of a modern shrimp trawler, have been reclassified as non-historic. Table 4 includes final
archaeological recommendations for each anomaly.

Probing
At the request of the project sponsor, 12 targets were investigated by divers in hopes that avoidance
restrictions could be minimized, thereby increasing the area of sediment available for environmental
restoration projects. Twelve magnetic anomalies, including 4 associated with sonar targets, were probed
by divers in July 2016. Probing disproved the presence of historic wrecks at 9 targets (Table 4). One of
those 9 targets, Mag 26 / Sonar 10, proved to be the mast, outriggers and netting from a modern shrimp

34 | P a g e

trawler. The trawlerâs hull was not discovered, although an area of buried wood nearby might be
associated with disarticulated superstructure, such as the wheelhouse. Buried wrecks were discovered
at 3 of the 12 targets that were probed. All of the buried wrecks are constructed of wood and are
presumed to be historic. Descriptions of target probing results are provided below near their respective
illustrations. A total of 37.5 acres of sediment was freed for environmental restoration by this effort.

Archaeological Assessment
Twenty six targets were initially recommended for avoidance, based on the geophysical survey results,
due to their potential association with historic shipwrecks. Seven of those targets were confirmed as
wrecks by sonar imagery, while another 6 targets were associated with ambiguous sonar contacts that
might indicate buried wreckage. Thirteen magnetic anomalies that lack sonar contacts also were
selected as potential shipwrecks. As a result of diver probing, 9 of the original 26 geophysical targets are
recommended for cultural resource clearance and 3 additional wrecks, plus partial remains of a shrimp
trawler, were discovered. Having completed probing investigations, there are now a total of 10 targets
known to be wrecked or abandoned vessels that are potentially historic, 7 targets remaining that are
potentially significant based on their magnetic anomalies, and 9 targets that will be recommended for
clearance from further archaeological concern, including 1 that seems comprised of superstructure
displaced from a modern shrimp trawler.
Wrecked or Abandoned Vessels
Ten wrecked or abandoned vessels have been discovered as a result of these combined geophysical
survey and probing investigations. Seven of those are visible as obvious wrecks on side-scan sonar
images. The fact that these vessels are preserved above the seabed indicates they are likely constructed
of metal. Three other wrecks were discovered buried beneath the seabed as a result of probing on
magnetic anomalies.
Dimensions of visible wrecks are provided in Table 3. They range in length from 46 to 115 ft and in width
from 14 to 40 ft. Targets Mag 1/Sonar 1 and Mag 2/Sonar 2 (figures 9 and 10) appear nearly identical,
although their dimensions differ slightly. These two targets are located quite close to one another, so it
seems plausible that they might have been abandoned at the same time. Likewise, Mag 15/Sonar 6 and
Mag 16/Sonar 7 (figures 12 and 13) are similar in appearance and located only a few hundred feet apart.
Mag 17/Sonar 8 and Mag 23/Sonar 9 (figures 14 and 15) both resemble rectangular barges. The barge at
Mag 23/Sonar 9 appears to have lost a deck load of rock off to one side, suggesting that it might date
from the period of jetty construction. Several other isolated piles of apparent rock also were observed in
the vicinity of the northern jetty. The wreck at Mag 9/Sonar 4 (Figure 11) appears unique among this
assemblage of vessels. It has a rectangular structure near its center that might be remains of an engine
block. All of the seven wrecks observed on sonar images are easily accessible to future researches and
might quickly provide diagnostic information as to their ages and functions.
Three buried wrecks were discovered by probing of anomalies. Mag 10, Mag 13, and Mag 24 (figures 1621) are associated with wooden wrecks of undetermined age and type. The vessel at Mag 10 (figures 1617 and Table 5) is approximately 40-65 ft wide x 85 ft long. It seems to have a mostly unbroken flat deck,
which is buried only 0.5- 2 ft making it quite accessible to future researchers. The width appears to
35 | P a g e

narrow toward one end, and its shape suggests that it might be a type of barge. Probing along the sides
indicates that at least 8 feet of vertical hull is present. In some places the side hull seems to be vertical.
In other places it seems to be slanted. Elevations of the âdeckâ are consistently in the 0.5-2-ft range,
indicating that the vessel is sitting relatively level, so any slanting of the side hull is likely designed into
its construction.
The wooden vessel buried at Mag 13 (figures 18-19 and Table 6) is relatively small with maximum
dimensions of roughly 13x35 ft. The boat is buried from 2-10 ft deep, suggesting that a substantial
amount of vertical hull is present. The vessel is drawn as double-ended in Figure 19 only for the purpose
of estimating its maximum dimensions. The actual shape is unknown. It would be very difficult to
determine its precise shape based on probing, particularly at the ends if they were, in fact, tapered.
The vessel buried at Mag 24 (figures 20-21 and Table 7) has estimated dimensions of 15x65-ft. Wood
was detected at depths ranging from 6-15 ft. Several probes struck wood at a depth of 14 feet below the
seabed, which is the limit of dredging for this project. The maximum depth of probing conducted by
divers was limited to 16 ft by the length of pipe used. It is possible that portions of this hull exist below
that depth and were undetected. The greater depth of burial of this vessel in comparison to the other
wrecks discovered by probing helps explain the relatively low amplitude of its magnetic anomaly.

Potentially Significant Magnetic Anomalies
The following 7 unidentified and uninvestigated targets (figures 22-28) remain on the list for
archaeological avoidance (in addition to the ten wrecks listed above) based on the fact that their
magnetic anomalies resemble those observed over verified shipwrecks (Gearhart 2001). Illustrations of
the following anomalies also compare favorably with the 10 verified shipwreck anomalies added to that
list based on this study (above).

Figure 28: Mag-20 Anomaly

54 | P a g e

Targets Cleared by Probing
Probing disproved the presence of historic wrecks at the following 9 targets (see also Table 4). Maps of
probing results are overlaid for each on their magnetic contours. A table of depths and materials
encountered at each probe is provided adjacent the corresponding illustration. One of the 9 targets
probed, Mag 26/Sonar 10, proved to be the mast, outriggers and netting from a modern shrimp trawler.
Fourteen feet of mast was exposed above the seabed, although it was lying at such an angle that it only
rises 9 ft above the bottom. Part of at least one outrigger was attached to the mast. A section of
electrical conduit attached to the mast appears to be made of plastic. Probing all around the mast to a
depth of 16 ft failed to make contact with wreckage of any kind. Shrimp nets were found buried 1.5-2.5
ft in two probes near the mast. The mast appears to be separated from the trawlerâs hull. An area of
buried wood was discovered 11 feet away from the mast. The wood measures at least 10x25 ft across
and was buried about 3 ft on average. The hull was not discovered. The area of buried wood is
suspected to be disarticulated superstructure, possibly part of a wheelhouse, moved here from another
location either intentionally or by a storm. The materials discovered at Mag 26/Sonar 10 are not
considered eligible for the NRHP based on their modern age and incomplete nature. Nevertheless, care
should be taken to protect the dredge from damage when moving sediment in the immediate vicinity of
this target.

Conclusions
The APE contains an unusually large sample of wrecks and potential wrecks, which might best be
attributed to a combination of the projectâs position at the seaward margin of the historic outer bar and
the likelihood that this area was used for abandonment of decommissioned vessels after completion of
the north jetty. A total of 7 wrecks are clearly visible on sonar imagery. All of these are expected to have
metallic hulls based on the fact that wooden hulls will not survive exposure in shallow waters of the Gulf
of Mexico for more than about 5 years. Three additional wrecks were discovered beneath the seabed by
probing investigations.
Several wrecks from Table 1 have been charted within the APE by the THC. While the accuracy of those
positions cannot be confirmed, they represent several plausible candidates for the identification of
wrecks discovered in the area, including USED Barge 42, a Houston Pilot Boat, and the tugs Joey,
Messenger, R.C. Viet, W.A. Wansley, and Willie. The loss dates for these vessels are unknown. Historic
charts suggest that several wrecks might have appeared in the APE between 1945 and 1951. No wrecks
were shown in the APE on the Galveston Bay Entrance chart in 1945 (USCGS 1945), but at least 7 wrecks
were charted in the survey area by 1951 (USCGS 1951). Nine âsubmerged wreckâ symbols and a 2,200 x
250-foot oblong area marked âWreckageâ are charted in or immediately adjacent the APE on the 2011
edition of NOAA Chart 11324 (Figure 1). Three of those symbols do not correlate with significant
geophysical targets. The other 6 submerged wreck symbols correlate closely with 11 significant targets,
including wrecks at Mag 1/Sonar 1 (Figure 9), Mag 2/Sonar 2 (Figure 10), Mag 10 (Figure 16), and Mag
13 (Figure 19), and potential wrecks at Mag 8/Sonar 3 (Figure 26) and Mag 11 (Figure 27) (USCGS 1950);
wrecks at Mag 15/Sonar 6 (Figure 12) and Mag 17/Sonar 8 (Figure 14) (USCGS 1951); a wreck at Mag
9/Sonar 4 (Figure 11) (U.S. Department of Commerce 1953); and a wreck at Mag 16/Sonar 7 (Figure 13)
(USCGS 1966).

Recommendations
Twenty six targets were originally recommended for avoidance based on the geophysical survey
reported above. Probing investigations conducted by divers disproved historic wrecks at 9 of those
locations, leaving a total of 17 targets to be avoided. Of those 17 targets, 10 are wrecked or abandoned
vessels, including 7 visible on sonar and 3 that are buried. Seven potentially significant magnetic
anomalies remain uninvestigated and are, therefore, still on the list for avoidance. Those 17 targets
(Table 4) are potentially historic shipwrecks thus could meet criteria for State Antiquities Landmark or
for the NRHP. SAM recommends avoidance of all 17 targets by bottom-disturbing activities associated
with this project (Mag 1/Sonar 1, Mag 2/Sonar 2, Mag 3, Mag 4, Mag 5/Sonar 15, Mag 7, Mag 8/Sonar 3,
Mag 9/Sonar 4, Mag 10, Mag 11, Mag 13, Mag 15/Sonar 6, Mag 16/Sonar 7, Mag 17/Sonar 8, Mag 20,
Mag 23/Sonar 9 and Mag 24). Avoidance distance is mandated by The Texas Administrative Code, Title
13, Part 2, Chapter 26, as 50 m (164 ft) beyond the target boundaries. In the event that shipwreck
remains, or other potentially historic materials, are discovered anywhere in the APE during the course of
construction, work should be halted within 50 m (164 ft) of the find until the THC can provide direction
concerning the discovery. SAM recommends cultural resource clearance for all portions of the APE
outside of avoidance polygons as illustrated in Appendix A.
